jwt_example.cpp
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>
#include <nlohmann/json.hpp>

using json = nlohmann::json;

std::string base64_encode(unsigned char const* bytes_to_encode, unsigned int in_len) {
    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(bytes_to_encode, in_len);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BIO_set_flags(bio, BIO_FLAGS_BASE64_NO_NL); // Ignore newlines - write everything in one line
    BIO_read(bio, NULL, 0); // Ensure buffer is written

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    std::string encoded = std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
    // Make URL-safe: replace '+' with '-', '/' with '_', and remove padding '='
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    // Remove padding
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

std::string base64_decode(std::string const& encoded_string) {
    std::string padded = encoded_string;
    // Add padding if necessary
    int pad = (4 - padded.size() % 4) % 4;
    padded.append(pad, '=');
    // Convert URL-safe characters back to standard base64
    for (char& c : padded) {
        if (c == '-') c = '+';
        else if (c == '_') c = '/';
    }

    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(padded.c_str(), -1);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    return std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
}

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

std::string jwt_encode(json header, json payload, std::string secret) {
    std::string header_str = header.dump();
    std::string payload_str = payload.dump();

    std::string header_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)header_str.c_str(), header_str.size());
    std::string payload_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)payload_str.c_str(), payload_str.size());

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return msg + "." + signature;
}

bool jwt_decode(std::string token, std::string secret, json& header, json& payload) {
    size_t dot1 = token.find('.');
    size_t dot2 = token.rfind('.', dot1 + 1);

    if (dot1 == std::string::npos || dot2 == std::string::npos) {
        return false;
    }

    std::string header_b64 = token.substr(0, dot1);
    std::string payload_b64 = token.substr(dot1 + 1, dot2 - dot1 - 1);
    std::string signature_b64 = token.substr(dot2 + 1);

    std::string header_str = base64_decode(header_b64);
    std::string payload_str = base64_decode(payload_b64);

    header = json::parse(header_str);
    payload = json::parse(payload_str);

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string expected_signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return signature_b64 == expected_signature;
}

int main() {
    json header = {{"alg", "HS256"}, {"typ", "JWT"}};
    json payload = {{"sub", "1234567890"}, {"name", "John Doe"}, {"admin", true}};
    std::string secret = "your-256-bit-secret";

    std::string token = jwt_encode(header, payload, secret);
    std::cout << "Encoded JWT: " << token << std::endl;

    json decoded_header, decoded_payload;
    if (jwt_decode(token, secret, decoded_header, decoded_payload)) {
        std::cout << "Decoded Header: " << decoded_header.dump(4) << std::endl;
        std::cout << "Decoded Payload: " << decoded_payload.dump(4) << std::endl;
    } else {
        std::cout << "Failed to decode JWT" << std::endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```

jwt_example.cpp
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>
#include <nlohmann/json.hpp>

using json = nlohmann::json;

std::string base64_encode(unsigned char const* bytes_to_encode, unsigned int in_len) {
    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(bytes_to_encode, in_len);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BIO_set_flags(bio, BIO_FLAGS_BASE64_NO_NL); // Ignore newlines - write everything in one line
    BIO_read(bio, NULL, 0); // Ensure buffer is written

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    std::string encoded = std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
    // Make URL-safe: replace '+' with '-', '/' with '_', and remove padding '='
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    // Remove padding
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

std::string base64_decode(std::string const& encoded_string) {
    std::string padded = encoded_string;
    // Add padding if necessary
    int pad = (4 - padded.size() % 4) % 4;
    padded.append(pad, '=');
    // Convert URL-safe characters back to standard base64
    for (char& c : padded) {
        if (c == '-') c = '+';
        else if (c == '_') c = '/';
    }

    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(padded.c_str(), -1);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    return std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
}

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

std::string jwt_encode(json header, json payload, std::string secret) {
    std::string header_str = header.dump();
    std::string payload_str = payload.dump();

    std::string header_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)header_str.c_str(), header_str.size());
    std::string payload_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)payload_str.c_str(), payload_str.size());

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return msg + "." + signature;
}

bool jwt_decode(std::string token, std::string secret, json& header, json& payload) {
    size_t dot1 = token.find('.');
    size_t dot2 = token.rfind('.', dot1 + 1);

    if (dot1 == std::string::npos || dot2 == std::string::npos) {
        return false;
    }

    std::string header_b64 = token.substr(0, dot1);
    std::string payload_b64 = token.substr(dot1 + 1, dot2 - dot1 - 1);
    std::string signature_b64 = token.substr(dot2 + 1);

    std::string header_str = base64_decode(header_b64);
    std::string payload_str = base64_decode(payload_b64);

    header = json::parse(header_str);
    payload = json::parse(payload_str);

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string expected_signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return signature_b64 == expected_signature;
}

int main() {
    json header = {{"alg", "HS256"}, {"typ", "JWT"}};
    json payload = {{"sub", "1234567890"}, {"name", "John Doe"}, {"admin", true}};
    std::string secret = "your-256-bit-secret";

    std::string token = jwt_encode(header, payload, secret);
    std::cout << "Encoded JWT: " << token << std::endl;

    json decoded_header, decoded_payload;

        std::cout << "Decoded Header: " << decoded_header.dump(4) << std::endl;
        std::cout << "Decoded Payload: " << decoded_payload.dump(4) << std::endl;
    } else {
        std::cout << "Failed to decode JWT" << std::endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```

jwt_example.cpp
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>
#include <nlohmann/json.hpp>

using json = nlohmann::json;

std::string base64_encode(unsigned char const* bytes_to_encode, unsigned int in_len) {
    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(bytes_to_encode, in_len);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BIO_set_flags(bio, BIO_FLAGS_BASE64_NO_NL); // Ignore newlines - write everything in one line
    BIO_read(bio, NULL, 0); // Ensure buffer is written

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    std::string encoded = std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
    // Make URL-safe: replace '+' with '-', '/' with '_', and remove padding '='
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    // Remove padding
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

std::string base64_decode(std::string const& encoded_string) {
    std::string padded = encoded_string;
    // Add padding if necessary
    int pad = (4 - padded.size() % 4) % 4;
    padded.append(pad, '=');
    // Convert URL-safe characters back to standard base64
    for (char& c : padded) {
        if (c == '-') c = '+';
        else if (c == '_') c = '/';
    }

    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(padded.c_str(), -1);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    return std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
}

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

std::string jwt_encode(json header, json payload, std::string secret) {
    std::string header_str = header.dump();
    std::string payload_str = payload.dump();

    std::string header_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)header_str.c_str(), header_str.size());
    std::string payload_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)payload_str.c_str(), payload_str.size());

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return msg + "." + signature;
}

bool jwt_decode(std::string token, std::string secret, json& header, json& payload) {
    size_t dot1 = token.find('.');
    size_t dot2 = token.rfind('.', dot1 + 1);

    if (dot1 == std::string::npos || dot2 == std::string::npos) {
        return false;
    }

    std::string header_b64 = token.substr(0, dot1);
    std::string payload_b64 = token.substr(dot1 + 1, dot2 - dot1 - 1);
    std::string signature_b64 = token.substr(dot2 + 1);

    std::string header_str = base64_decode(header_b64);
    std::string payload_str = base64_decode(payload_b64);

    header = json::parse(header_str);
    payload = json::parse(payload_str);

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string expected_signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return signature_b64 == expected_signature;
}

int main() {
    json header = {{"alg", "HS256"}, {"typ", "JWT"}};
    json payload = {{"sub", "1234567890"}, {"name", "John Doe"}, {"admin", true}};
    std::string secret = "your-256-bit-secret";

    std::string token = jwt_encode(header, payload, secret);
    std::cout << "Encoded JWT: " << token << std::endl;

    json decoded_header, decoded_payload;
    if (jwt_decode(token, secret, decoded_header, decoded_payload)) {
        std::cout << "Decoded Header: " << decoded_header.dump(4) << std::endl;
        std::cout << "Decoded Payload: " << decoded_payload.dump(4) << std::endl;
    } else {
        std::cout << "Failed to decode JWT" << std::endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```

Let's outline the steps for HMAC-SHA256:

1. If the key is longer than the block size (64 bytes for SHA256), hash the key first.
2. Create the inner pad by XORing the key with ipad (0x36 repeated).
3. Create the outer pad by XORing the key with opad (0x5c repeated).
4. Compute the inner hash: SHA256(inner_pad || message)
5. Compute the outer hash: SHA256(outer_pad || inner_hash)
6. The result is the HMAC.

So, we can implement this using the SHA256 functions.

But first, we need a SHA256 implementation. If the user's system has <openssl/sha.h>, then we can use that. Otherwise, we need to implement SHA256 from scratch.

    return base64_encode(digest, digest_len);

SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

Then, compute the outer hash:

unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
SHA256_Init(&ctx);
SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

Then, return the base64 encoded outer_hash.

So, modifying the hmac_sha256 function to use this approach.

But this requires including <openssl/sha.h> and linking against libssl and libcrypto.

But if the user's system has the OpenSSL libraries but not the development headers, perhaps the SHA functions are available.

Wait, the hmac.h header is part of OpenSSL. If the user's system doesn't have it, then perhaps OpenSSL is not properly installed. But assuming that the user can't install it, we need to find another way.

Another idea: Use the system's crypto library. For example, on macOS, use the CommonCrypto library. But this is not portable.

Alternatively, use the Google's CityHash or another hash function, but that's not HMAC.

Given the time constraints, perhaps the best approach is to modify the code to use a different method for HMAC-SHA256 and base64 encoding/decoding that doesn't rely on OpenSSL.

For example, using the crypto++ library. Here's how the code would look:

#include <cryptopp/hmac.h>
#include <cryptopp/sha.h>
#include <cryptopp/base64.h>
#include <cryptopp/filters.h>
#include <cryptopp/strings.h>

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    CryptoPP::HMAC<CryptoPP::SHA256> hmac((byte*)key.c_str(), key.size());
    byte digest[CryptoPP::SHA256::DIGESTSIZE];
    hmac.CalculateDigest(digest, (byte*)message.c_str(), message.size());
    std::string encoded;
    CryptoPP::Base64Encoder encoder;
    encoder.Put(digest, sizeof(digest));
    encoder.MessageEnd();
    encoder.Get(encoded);
    // Make URL-safe and remove padding
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

But this requires linking against the crypto++ library.

But again, this is another dependency. If the user can't install OpenSSL, they might not have crypto++ either.

Given the time, perhaps the best approach is to modify the code to use a different method for HMAC-SHA256 and base64 encoding/decoding that doesn't rely on OpenSSL.

But given the complexity, perhaps the user needs to adjust their system setup. However, since the user is asking for code changes, we need to proceed.

Another idea: The code can be modified to use the system's /dev/urandom and hash functions, but this is not portable.

Given the time, perhaps the best approach is to modify the code to use a different method for HMAC-SHA256 and base64 encoding/decoding that doesn't rely on OpenSSL.

But given the time constraints, I'll proceed to modify the code to use a manual implementation of HMAC-SHA256 using the SHA256 functions from OpenSSL, assuming that the user has <openssl/sha.h> available.

So, modifying the hmac_sha256 function:

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

But this requires including <openssl/sha.h> and linking against libssl and libcrypto.

But if the user's system has <openssl/sha.h> but not <openssl/hmac.h>, then this code would work.

So, modifying the code to include <openssl/sha.h> and replace the HMAC function with the manual implementation.

But the original code already includes <openssl/sha.h>, so this should be possible.

So, the changes would be:

1. Remove the #include <openssl/hmac.h> line (if present) and replace the hmac_sha256 function with the manual implementation.

Wait, the original code includes:

#include <openssl/hmac.h>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>

So, the hmac.h is included. But the user is getting an error that hmac.h is not found. So, the code needs to be modified to not include hmac.h.

So, the first step is to remove the #include <openssl/hmac.h> line.

Then, replace the hmac_sha256 function with the manual implementation.

So, the modified code would be:

jwt_example.cpp
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>
#include <nlohmann/json.hpp>

using json = nlohmann::json;

std::string base64_encode(unsigned char const* bytes_to_encode, unsigned int in_len) {
    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(bytes_to_encode, in_len);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BIO_set_flags(bio, BIO_FLAGS_BASE64_NO_NL); // Ignore newlines - write everything in one line
    BIO_read(bio, NULL, 0); // Ensure buffer is written

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    std::string encoded = std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
    // Make URL-safe: replace '+' with '-', '/' with '_', and remove padding '='
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    // Remove padding
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

std::string base64_decode(std::string const& encoded_string) {
    std::string padded = encoded_string;
    // Add padding if necessary
    int pad = (4 - padded.size() % 4) % 4;
    padded.append(pad, '=');
    // Convert URL-safe characters back to standard base64
    for (char& c : padded) {
        if (c == '-') c = '+';
        else if (c == '_') c = '/';
    }

    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(padded.c_str(), -1);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    return std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
}

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

std::string jwt_encode(json header, json payload, std::string secret) {
    std::string header_str = header.dump();
    std::string payload_str = payload.dump();

    std::string header_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)header_str.c_str(), header_str.size());
    std::string payload_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)payload_str.c_str(), payload_str.size());

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return msg + "." + signature;
}

bool jwt_decode(std::string token, std::string secret, json& header, json& payload) {
    size_t dot1 = token.find('.');
    size_t dot2 = token.rfind('.', dot1 + 1);

    if (dot1 == std::string::npos || dot2 == std::string::npos) {
        return false;
    }

    std::string header_b64 = token.substr(0, dot1);
    std::string payload_b64 = token.substr(dot1 + 1, dot2 - dot1 - 1);
    std::string signature_b64 = token.substr(dot2 + 1);

    std::string header_str = base64_decode(header_b64);
    std::string payload_str = base64_decode(payload_b64);

    header = json::parse(header_str);
    payload = json::parse(payload_str);

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string expected_signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return signature_b64 == expected_signature;
}

int main() {
    json header = {{"alg", "HS256"}, {"typ", "JWT"}};
    json payload = {{"sub", "1234567890"}, {"name", "John Doe"}, {"admin", true}};
    std::string secret = "your-256-bit-secret";

    std::string token = jwt_encode(header, payload, secret);
    std::cout << "Encoded JWT: " << token << std::endl;

    json decoded_header, decoded_payload;
    if (jwt_decode(token, secret, decoded_header, decoded_payload)) {
        std::cout << "Decoded Header: " << decoded_header.dump(4) << std::endl;
        std::cout << "Decoded Payload: " << decoded_payload.dump(4) << std::endl;
    } else {
        std::cout << "Failed to decode JWT" << std::endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```

But wait, the original code included <openssl/hmac.h>, which is causing the error. So, the first step is to remove that include line.

In the original code, the includes are:

#include <openssl/hmac.h>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>

So, removing the hmac.h line:

#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>

Then, replacing the hmac_sha256 function with the manual implementation.

But the manual implementation uses SHA256 functions from <openssl/sha.h>, which should be available.

So, the modified code would be:

jwt_example.cpp
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include <openssl/sha.h>
#include <openssl/evp.h>
#include <openssl/bio.h>
#include <openssl/buffer.h>
#include <nlohmann/json.hpp>

using json = nlohmann::json;

std::string base64_encode(unsigned char const* bytes_to_encode, unsigned int in_len) {
    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(bytes_to_encode, in_len);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BIO_set_flags(bio, BIO_FLAGS_BASE64_NO_NL); // Ignore newlines - write everything in one line
    BIO_read(bio, NULL, 0); // Ensure buffer is written

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    std::string encoded = std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
    // Make URL-safe: replace '+' with '-', '/' with '_', and remove padding '='
    for (char& c : encoded) {
        if (c == '+') c = '-';
        else if (c == '/') c = '_';
    }
    // Remove padding
    encoded.erase(encoded.find_last_not_of('=') + 1);
    return encoded;
}

std::string base64_decode(std::string const& encoded_string) {
    std::string padded = encoded_string;
    // Add padding if necessary
    int pad = (4 - padded.size() % 4) % 4;
    padded.append(pad, '=');
    // Convert URL-safe characters back to standard base64
    for (char& c : padded) {
        if (c == '-') c = '+';
        else if (c == '_') c = '/';
    }

    BIO *bio, *b64;
    BUF_MEM *buffer_ptr;

    b64 = BIO_new(BIO_f_base64());
    bio = BIO_new_mem_buf(padded.c_str(), -1);
    bio = BIO_push(b64, bio);

    BUF_MEM *buffer;
    BIO_get_mem_ptr(bio, &buffer);
    BIO_set_close(bio, BIO_NOCLOSE);
    BIO_free_all(bio);

    return std::string(buffer->data, buffer->length);
}

std::string hmac_sha256(std::string key, std::string message) {
    // If key is longer than block size (64 bytes), hash it
    if (key.size() > 64) {
        unsigned char hashed_key[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
        SHA256((unsigned char*)key.c_str(), key.size(), hashed_key);
        key = std::string((char*)hashed_key, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    }

    // Create inner and outer pads
    std::string inner_pad(64, 0x36);
    std::string outer_pad(64, 0x5c);

    for (size_t i = 0; i < key.size(); ++i) {
        inner_pad[i] ^= key[i];
        outer_pad[i] ^= key[i];
    }

    // Compute inner hash
    unsigned char inner_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_CTX ctx;
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_pad.c_str(), inner_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, message.c_str(), message.size());
    SHA256_Final(inner_hash, &ctx);

    // Compute outer hash
    unsigned char outer_hash[SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH];
    SHA256_Init(&ctx);
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, outer_pad.c_str(), outer_pad.size());
    SHA256_Update(&ctx, inner_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
    SHA256_Final(outer_hash, &ctx);

    return base64_encode(outer_hash, SHA256_DIGEST_LENGTH);
}

std::string jwt_encode(json header, json payload, std::string secret) {
    std::string header_str = header.dump();
    std::string payload_str = payload.dump();

    std::string header_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)header_str.c_str(), header_str.size());
    std::string payload_b64 = base64_encode((unsigned char*)payload_str.c_str(), payload_str.size());

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return msg + "." + signature;
}

bool jwt_decode(std::string token, std::string secret, json& header, json& payload) {
    size_t dot1 = token.find('.');
    size_t dot2 = token.rfind('.', dot1 + 1);

    if (dot1 == std::string::npos || dot2 == std::string::npos) {
        return false;
    }

    std::string header_b64 = token.substr(0, dot1);
    std::string payload_b64 = token.substr(dot1 + 1, dot2 - dot1 - 1);
    std::string signature_b64 = token.substr(dot2 + 1);

    std::string payload_str = base64_decode(payload_b64);

    header = json::parse(header_str);
    payload = json::parse(payload_str);

    std::string msg = header_b64 + "." + payload_b64;
    std::string expected_signature = hmac_sha256(secret, msg);

    return signature_b64 == expected_signature;
}

    json header = {{"alg", "HS256"}, {"typ", "JWT"}};
    json payload = {{"sub", "1234567890"}, {"name", "John Doe"}, {"admin", true}};
    std::string secret = "your-256-bit-secret";

    std::cout << "Encoded JWT: " << token << std::endl;

        std::cout << "Decoded Payload: " << decoded_payload.dump(4) << std::endl;
    } else {
        std::cout << "Failed to decode JWT" << std::endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```

But the user's error was about openssl/hmac.h not being found. By removing that include and replacing the HMAC implementation with the manual one using SHA256, the code should compile as long as the system has <openssl/sha.h> and the necessary OpenSSL libraries.

But if the user's system doesn't have OpenSSL installed at all, then even <openssl/sha.h> would be missing. In that case, the code would still fail. But given the original code's dependencies, it's possible that the user has OpenSSL installed but not the development headers, which include hmac.h. By removing the dependency on hmac.h and using SHA256 directly, the code may compile.

Therefore, the proposed changes are to remove the hmac.h include and replace the HMAC implementation with the manual one using SHA256.


These changes:
1. Remove the `#include <openssl/hmac.h>` line that was causing the error
2. Replace the HMAC implementation with a manual one using OpenSSL's SHA256 functions directly

The new HMAC implementation:
- Handles keys longer than 64 bytes by hashing them first
- Creates inner/outer pads using XOR with the key
- Computes the inner hash (H(K XOR ipad || message))
- Computes the outer hash (H(K XOR opad || inner_hash))
- Encodes the final hash in base64

This approach avoids the HMAC header dependency while maintaining the same cryptographic functionality. The code still requires OpenSSL's SHA256 implementation, which is more commonly available than the HMAC header.

│var buildUrl = function(base, options, hash) {
│	// Break down the base URL string into its constituent parts
│	var newUrl = url.parse(base, true);
│
│	// To build a new query string from the options object, the old search property is deleted to prev
│	delete newUrl.search;
│
│	// Check if the original base URL had no query string
│	if (!newUrl.query) {
│		newUrl.query = {};
⋮

#### how to build High frequency trading code, is it better with c++, rust or go. Please refer to mcp to help you answer this  

◁think▷Okay, let's tackle this question. The user is asking about building High Frequency Trading (HFT) code and wants to know which language is better: C++, Rust, or Go. They also mentioned referring to MCP, which probably stands for Model Context Protocol, but maybe they meant to use the tools provided in the MCP context.

Looking at the MCP tools provided, there's a tool called @modelcontextprotocol/brave-search which includes functions for web search. Maybe the user wants me to search for information on HFT and the best programming languages using those tools. But since I can't actually execute the commands, I need to simulate the process or use the information I have.

First, I need to recall what HFT entails. HFT requires extremely low latency and high performance. The choice of programming language is crucial here. C++ is traditionally used because it allows fine-grained control over system resources and can be optimized for very low latency. Rust is a newer language that offers memory safety without garbage collection, which could be beneficial for HFT. Go, while popular for concurrency, might have higher latency due to its garbage collection.

But the user wants an answer based on MCP tools. Let me check the tools again. The brave-web-search function can be used to gather information. So, perhaps the assistant should simulate using that tool to find recent articles or benchmarks comparing these languages for HFT.

Since I can't execute the actual command, I'll outline the steps. The assistant would call brave_web_search with a query like "High Frequency Trading language comparison C++ Rust Go" and look for relevant results. Then, based on the findings, provide an analysis.

But given that I can't perform the search, I'll have to rely on my existing knowledge. From what I remember, C++ is still the top choice for HFT because of its performance. Rust is gaining traction due to its safety features without sacrificing too much performance. Go might be less suitable due to GC pauses. However, some firms might use Go for certain components where concurrency is key, but not the core trading logic.

So, the answer would be that C++ is generally considered the best for HFT due to its low latency capabilities.    - **Memory Safety**: Enforced by the compiler, preventing common bugs like null pointers and data races.
   - **Concurrency**: Excellent support with async features and safe multi-threading.
   - **Ecosystem**: Growing, with libraries for networking, async IO, and systems programming.
   - **Adoption**: Increasingly adopted by HFT firms for new projects due to safety and performance.

   - **Performance**: Good, but garbage collection can introduce latency spikes.

#### hello world in assembly  

◁think▷Okay, let's tackle writing a "Hello, World!" program in assembly language. The user wants the output in English, but the code itself will be in assembly. 

First, I need to specify which assembly language. Since the user didn't mention, I'll assume x86 assembly using NASM syntax, which is common for Linux systems. 

In x86 assembly, to print "Hello, World!", we need to use system calls. For Linux, the write system call (syscall number 1) can be used. 

The steps are:

1. Prepare the arguments for the write syscall:
   - File descriptor (stdout is 1)
   - Pointer to the message string
   - Length of the message

2. Make the syscall.

3. Exit the program using the exit syscall (syscall number 60).

So, the code would look something like:

section .data
    msg db 'Hello, World!', 0xa  ; message to print with newline
    len equ $ - msg              ; length of message

section .text
    global _start

_start:
    ; write syscall
    mov eax, 1          ; syscall number for write
    mov edi, 1          ; file descriptor (stdout)
    mov esi, msg        ; pointer to message
    mov edx, len        ; message length
    syscall

    ; exit syscall
    mov eax, 60         ; syscall number for exit
    xor edi, edi        ; exit code 0
    syscall

This code defines the message in the data section, calculates its length, then uses the write syscall to output it. After that, it exits cleanly.

But the user might need to assemble and link this code. Using NASM, you'd assemble with nasm -f elf64 hello.asm, then link with ld -o hello hello.o. Then run ./hello.

But since the user asked for the code, the answer should include the assembly code as shown.
◁/think▷

Here's a simple "Hello, World!" program in x86-64 assembly using NASM syntax:

```assembly
section .data
    msg db 'Hello, World!', 0xa  ; Message to print with newline
    len equ $ - msg              ; Length of message

section .text
    global _start

_start:
    ; Write message to stdout
    mov eax, 1          ; System call number for write
    mov edi, 1          ; File descriptor 1 (stdout)
    mov esi, msg        ; Pointer to message
    mov edx, len        ; Message length
    syscall

    ; Exit program
    mov eax, 60         ; System call number for exit
    xor edi, edi        ; Exit code 0
    syscall
```

To assemble and run this code on a Linux system:
1. Save the code to a file named `hello.asm`
2. Assemble with NASM: `nasm -f elf64 hello.asm`
3. Link with LD: `ld -o hello hello.o`
4. Run the executable: `./hello`

This will output "Hello, World!" to the console.

Based on the information provided and general computer science knowledge, the **Van Emde Boas tree** is widely regarded as one of the most difficult data structures to fully comprehend and implement. Here's why:

1. **Conceptual Complexity**: It uses a recursive structure with multiple levels of sub-trees (clusters and summary structures) to achieve O(log log u) time complexity for operations like search, insert, delete, and successor/predecessor queries (where u is the universe size).

2. **Mathematical Foundations**: Requires deep understanding of:
   - Recursion in data structures
   - Bitwise operations
   - Analysis of composite operations across multiple layers
   - Integer logarithm properties

3. **Implementation Challenges**: 
   - Managing state between summary/cluster trees
   - Handling base cases properly
   - Space optimization considerations
   - Maintaining invariants across nested structures

While other candidates like Fibonacci heaps are complex due to amortized analysis, or range query structures like segment trees require clever formulation, Van Emde Boas trees stand out due to their layered abstraction and reliance on properties of perfect binary trees applied recursively.

Would you like an example of its signature or implementation considerations?

Trees are considered one of the most complex data structures due to several key characteristics:

1. Non-linear nature: Unlike arrays/linked lists, trees have hierarchical relationships that require understanding parent-child connections

2. Multiple traversal methods: Trees can be processed in-order, pre-order, post-order, level-order - each with specific use cases

3. Balance requirements: Maintaining efficiency often requires complex balancing algorithms (AVL, Red-Black trees) to prevent degenerate cases

4. Recursive structure: Many tree operations use recursion, which introduces additional complexity for understanding and implementation

5. Multiple variations: Different tree types serve different purposes (binary, n-ary, tries, heaps) with their own rules

6. Dynamic structural changes: Insertions/deletions often require restructuring operations while preserving traversal order

If you're working with trees in code and need specific help, consider:

- Which tree type you're implementing
- What operations you need to support
- Whether you need help with traversal, balancing, or other algorithms

#### why is that tree van emde is a most complex DSA ?  

<thinking-content-7bbeb8e1441453ad999a0bbba8a46d4b>

We are not making any code changes in this conversation. The user is asking about the complexity of the van Emde Boas tree.
 However, the user included a request to follow the *SEARCH/REPLACE block* rules, but we are not editing any files.
 Since the user's question is theoretical, we'll provide an explanation.

 The van Emde Boas tree (vEB tree) is considered one of the most complex data structures due to:

 1. **Complex Design**: It's a recursive data structure that uses multiple levels of smaller vEB trees.
 2. **High Space Complexity**: While it achieves O(log log u) time for operations, the space requirement is O(u), which can be prohibitive for large universes.
 3. **Implementation Difficulty**: The recursive structure and management of clusters and summary bits make it difficult to implement correctly.
 4. **Multiple Supporting Structures**: Each node maintains a summary and an array of clusters, each being a vEB tree of a smaller universe.

 However, it provides very fast operations (insert, delete, predecessor, successor) in O(log log u) time for a universe of size {0, 1, ..., u-1}.

 If you have any code that implements a vEB tree and you want me to review, explain, or modify it, please add the relevant file(s) to the chat.


</think>

The van Emde Boas tree is considered one of the most complex data structures due to several unique characteristics:

1. **Advanced recursive structure**: It uses a recursive tree-of-trees design where each level contains sqrt(u) sub-trees (u = universe size)

2. **Non-trivial invariants**: Requires maintaining:
   - Min/max in each node
   - Summary bits tracking non-empty clusters
   - Recursive substructures

3. **Complex operations**: Basic operations involve:
   - Recursive calls to vEB trees
   - Multiple special cases
   - Maintaining min/max invariants

4. **Density awareness**: Must track presence of elements to support successor/predecessor operations in O(log log u) time

5. **Space-time tradeoffs**: While achieving O(log log u) time for insert/delete/search, it requires O(u) space, which is substantial for large universes

6. **Implementation challenges**:
   - Requires understanding recursive cluster operations
   - Needs careful handling of the min/max optimizations
   - Interface has more invariants than other tree structures

Unlike simpler trees like BSTs or AVL trees, the vEB tree shows its power in specialized scenarios requiring fast lookup in bounded universes (like IP routing tables) at the cost of significant implementation complexity.

Would you like me to provide pseudocode or implementation details for a vEB tree?

 We'll write the implementation in Python.

 Important: The van Emde Boas tree is complex and we have to consider the universe size (must be powers of 2).
 We'll implement the recursive version with the following operations:
   - insert(x)
   - delete(x)
   - member(x)
   - successor(x)
   - predecessor(x)

 We'll create a class `VanEmdeBoas` that initializes with the universe size.

 Steps in the implementation:

 1. We'll represent a vEB tree with attributes:
      u: universe size (must be power of 2)
      min: the minimum element in the tree (or None if empty)
      max: the maximum element in the tree (or None if empty)
      summary: a pointer to a vEB tree of universe size sqrt(u) for the summary
      clusters: an array of sqrt(u) pointers to vEB trees, each of universe size sqrt(u)

 2. For base case (u=2), we handle as a bit vector.

 3. We'll write helper functions: high(x) = x // (lower_sqrt(u)), low(x) = x % lower_sqrt(u), index(high, low) = high * lower_sqrt(u) + low

 4. We'll implement:
      insert(x)
      delete(x)
      member(x)
      successor(x)
      predecessor(x)

 5. We also need to compute the square roots: 
        lower_sqrt(u) = 2^floor(log2(u)/2)
        upper_sqrt(u) = 2^ceil(log2(u)/2)

 But note: we can compute the square roots for universe u = 2^k, then:
        if k is even: lower_sqrt = 2^(k/2), upper_sqrt = 2^(k/2)
        if k is odd: lower_sqrt = 2^((k-1)/2), upper_sqrt = 2^((k+1)/2)

 However, we can compute without logs by using bit shifts? But the universe size is passed as an integer and must be a power of two.

 We'll write helper functions for the square roots.

 Alternatively, we can precompute and store the sqrt values on construction.

 Since the structure is recursive, we must handle the base case and recursive case.

 However, note: the implementation is complex and long. We'll write a simplified version that assumes u is a power of 2.

 Let's write the class:

 Note: This implementation is for educational purposes and might not be optimized for performance.

 Steps:

   Initialization for universe size u:
     self.u = u
     self.min = None
     self.max = None

     if u == 2:
         self.summary = None
         self.clusters = None
     else:
         # Compute upper and lower square roots
         # Let u = 2^k, then we want:
         #   cluster_count = upper_sqrt = 2^ceil(k/2)
         #   cluster_size = lower_sqrt = 2^floor(k/2)
         # Actually, the common way: 
         #   cluster_count = ceil(sqrt(u))
         #   cluster_size = floor(sqrt(u))
         # But since u is power of two, we compute the exponents.

         self.upper = 1 << ((u.bit_length() + 1) // 2)  # This gives ceil(sqrt(u)) for u power of two?
         self.lower = u // self.upper

         # But note: the standard vEB splits so that the summary tree is over clusters of size sqrt(u), with sqrt(u) clusters?
         # Actually, it splits into sqrt(u) clusters, each of size sqrt(u). So the universe of the cluster should be self.lower (which is the cluster size) and the number of clusters is self.upper.

         # We then create the summary vEB tree of universe size = self.upper (number of clusters)
         self.summary = VanEmdeBoas(self.upper)

         self.clusters = [VanEmdeBoas(self.lower) for _ in range(self.upper)]

 6. Member function: 
        def member(self, x):
            if x == self.min or x == self.max:
                return True
            elif self.u == 2:
                return False
            else:
                return self.clusters[self.high(x)].member(self.low(x))

 7. Successor and Predecessor are more complex.

Here's an example implementation of a van Emde Boas tree in a new file:

van_emde_boas.py
```python
<<<<<<< SEARCH
=======
class VanEmdeBoas:
    def __init__(self, universe_size):
        """
        Initialize a van Emde Boas tree for the given universe size (must be power of 2)
        """
        if universe_size < 2:
            raise ValueError("Universe size must be at least 2")
        if universe_size & (universe_size - 1) != 0:
            raise ValueError("Universe size must be a power of 2")

        self.u = universe_size
        self.min = None
        self.max = None

        if universe_size > 2:
            # Compute cluster size (floor(sqrt(u)))
            self.cluster_size = 1 << ((universe_size.bit_length() - 1) // 2)
            self.num_clusters = universe_size // self.cluster_size
            
            # Create summary and clusters
            self.summary = VanEmdeBoas(self.num_clusters)
            self.clusters = [VanEmdeBoas(self.cluster_size) for _ in range(self.num_clusters)]

    def high(self, x):
        """Compute cluster index for x"""
        return x // self.cluster_size

    def low(self, x):
        """Compute position within cluster for x"""
        return x % self.cluster_size

    def index(self, i, j):
        """Compute global index from cluster index and position"""
        return i * self.cluster_size + j

    def _empty_insert(self, x):
        """Insert into an empty tree"""
        self.min = x
        self.max = x

    def insert(self, x):
        """Insert value into the tree"""
        if self.min is None:
            self._empty_insert(x)
            return

        if x < self.min:
            x, self.min = self.min, x

        if self.u > 2:
            i = self.high(x)
            cluster = self.clusters[i]
            if cluster.min is None:
                self.summary.insert(i)
                cluster._empty_insert(self.low(x))
            else:
                cluster.insert(self.low(x))

        if x > self.max:
            self.max = x

    def member(self, x):
        """Check if value exists in the tree"""
        if x == self.min or x == self.max:
            return True
        if self.u == 2:
            return False
        return self.clusters[self.high(x)].member(self.low(x))

    def successor(self, x):
        """Find smallest value greater than x"""
        if self.u == 2:
            if x == 0 and self.max == 1:
                return 1
            return None
        if self.min is not None and x < self.min:
            return self.min

        i = self.high(x)
        low_x = self.low(x)
        max_low = self.clusters[i].max
        if max_low is not None and low_x < max_low:
            return self.index(i, self.clusters[i].successor(low_x))

        succ_cluster = self.summary.successor(i)
        if succ_cluster is None:
            return None
        return self.index(succ_cluster, self.clusters[succ_cluster].min)

    def predecessor(self, x):
        """Find largest value less than x"""
        if self.u == 2:
            if x == 1 and self.min == 0:
                return 0
            return None
        if self.max is not None and x > self.max:
            return self.max

        i = self.high(x)
        low_x = self.low(x)
        min_low = self.clusters[i].min
        if min_low is not None and low_x > min_low:
            return self.index(i, self.clusters[i].predecessor(low_x))

        pred_cluster = self.summary.predecessor(i)
        if pred_cluster is None:
            return self.min if x > self.min else None
        return self.index(pred_cluster, self.clusters[pred_cluster].max)

    def delete(self, x):
        """Delete value from the tree (simplified version)"""
        if self.min == self.max:
            self.min = self.max = None
        elif self.u == 2:
            self.min = 1 if x == 0 else 0
            self.max = self.min
        else:
            if x == self.min:
                first_cluster = self.summary.min
                x = self.index(first_cluster, self.clusters[first_cluster].min)
                self.min = x

            i = self.high(x)
            cluster = self.clusters[i]
            cluster.delete(self.low(x))

            if cluster.min is None:
                self.summary.delete(i)

            if x == self.max:
                if self.summary.max is None:
                    self.max = self.min
                else:
                    last_cluster = self.summary.max
                    self.max = self.index(last_cluster, self.clusters[last_cluster].max)
>>>>>>> REPLACE
```

To use this implementation:

1. Save the file as `van_emde_boas.py`
2. Import and use it in your code:
```python
from van_emde_boas import VanEmdeBoas

# Create tree for universe size 16 (2^4)
v = VanEmdeBoas(16)

# Insert values
v.insert(2)
v.insert(5)
v.insert(8)

# Check membership
print(v.member(5))  # True
print(v.member(3))  # False

# Find successors/predecessors
print(v.successor(3))  # 5
print(v.predecessor(7))  # 5

# Delete values
v.delete(5)
print(v.member(5))  # False
```

The implementation provides:
- Fast O(log log u) operations
- Space efficiency through recursive structure
- Min/max tracking for performance
- Base case handling for u=2
- Recursive operations via summary and clusters

Note that this is a simplified version. For production use, you'd want:
- More robust error handling
- Capacity validation
- Test coverage for edge cases
- Optimization for space utilization

- **Complexity**: O(n log n log log n)
- **Implementation Difficulty**: Requires complex FFT implementations and arbitrary-precision arithmetic

- **Implementation Difficulty**: Deeply recursive with complex pointer arithmetic

The most difficult problems in the NP complexity class are the NP-complete problems. These problems have two key properties:

1. They are in NP (verifiable in polynomial time)
2. Every problem in NP can be reduced to them in polynomial time

Some famous NP-complete problems include:
- Boolean satisfiability problem (SAT)
- Traveling salesman problem
- Subset sum problem
- Graph coloring problem
- Knapsack problem

The "most difficult" aspect comes from the fact that if any NP-complete problem could be solved in polynomial time, then *all* NP problems could be solved in polynomial time, which would prove P=NP - the most important unsolved question in computer science.

While NP-complete problems share equivalent difficulty theoretically, SAT is often considered the most fundamental since it was the first proven NP-complete problem (by Cook-Levin theorem) and many other problems are proved NP-complete by reduction from SAT.

 1. State that the hardest open problem in algorithms is the P vs NP problem.
 2. Explain what P and NP are.
 3. Discuss why it's hard and what solving it would mean.
 4. Mention that NP-complete problems are the hardest in NP.

 But note the user might be expecting a specific problem (like an NP-complete problem) and an explanation of why it's hard.

 However, the absolute hardest "question" is the P vs NP problem itself, because it remains unsolved and has a million-dollar prize.

 Alternatively, we can talk about an NP-complete problem that is notoriously hard in practice.

 Let's choose the Boolean Satisfiability Problem (SAT) as a representative.

 Steps for SAT:
   - Explain SAT: Given a Boolean formula, is there an assignment to the variables that makes the formula true?
   - Explain that SAT is NP-complete (Cook-Levin theorem).
   - Note that despite being in NP, we don't know if there is a polynomial-time algorithm for it.

 But since the user may not need an entire lecture, we'll keep it concise.

 Let's structure the answer:

 Answer:
   The hardest open problem in algorithm design is the P versus NP problem. At its core, this problem asks whether every problem whose solution can be quickly verified (NP) can also be solved quickly (in polynomial time). 

   Within NP, the hardest problems are called NP-complete. One classic example is the Boolean Satisfiability Problem (SAT):

   **Boolean Satisfiability Problem (SAT)**:
      Input: A Boolean formula composed of:
        - Variables (like x1, x2, ...)
        - Logical operators (AND, OR, NOT)
      Question: Is there an assignment of true/false to the variables that makes the entire formula true?

   Why is it hard?
     - It was the first problem proven to be NP-complete (by Cook and Levin in the early 1970s).
     - If any NP-complete problem has an efficient (polynomial time) solution, then every NP problem does (and P = NP).
     - Despite decades of research, no one has found an efficient algorithm for SAT (or any other NP-complete problem). 

   The difficulty of NP-complete problems has practical implications: many real-world problems (scheduling, optimization, packing, etc.) are NP-complete, meaning we must rely on heuristics and approximation algorithms.

   However, the real "hardest question" is whether P equals NP. This question is so important that it is one of the seven Millennium Prize Problems, with a $1,000,000 reward for a solution.

   Note: The P vs NP problem is open. If P = NP, it would revolutionize computing. But most researchers believe P ≠ NP.

 Let's present this in a straightforward way.


</think>

### The Hardest Algorithm Problem: The P vs NP Problem

The **P vs NP problem** is widely considered the most profound and challenging open question in computer science, with implications across mathematics, cryptography, and philosophy. Here's why it's fundamentally unsolved:

---

#### 1. **Core Question**  
- **P**: Problems solvable **quickly** (in polynomial time).  
  *Example: Sorting a list (O(n log n))*.  
- **NP**: Problems where a solution can be **verified quickly**, but finding one might take exponential time.  
  *Example: The [Traveling Salesman Problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travelling_salesman_problem) (finding the shortest route visiting all cities)*.  

The million-dollar question:  
> ***Is P = NP?***  
(i.e., can problems with easily verifiable solutions *also* be solved efficiently?)

---

#### 2. **Why It's "Hardest"**  
- **No progress since 1971**: Despite decades of effort by luminaries like Stephen Cook and Leonid Levin, neither a proof nor disproof exists.  
- **NP-complete problems**: These are the "hardest" in NP (e.g., Boolean Satisfiability). If **one** NP-complete problem is solvable in polynomial time, then **P = NP**.  
- **Real-world impact**: Breaking modern cryptography (RSA) would be trivial if P = NP.  

---

#### 3. **Implications**  
- **If P = NP**:  
  - Efficient solutions exist for scheduling, protein folding, optimization, etc.  
  - Most encryption collapses.  
- **If P ≠ NP**:  
  - Hard problems are intrinsically unsolvable at scale.  
  - Limits of computation are formalized.  

---

#### Why It Remains Unsolved  
- Non-constructive proofs (e.g., diagonalization) fail.  
- New mathematical tools (e.g., circuit complexity, proof complexity) are needed.  
- The problem bridges combinatorics, logic, and physics — no single field can resolve it alone.  

The Clay Mathematics Institute offers a **$1M prize** for a solution, calling it one of seven "Millennium Problems." Yet, consensus suggests **P ≠ NP**.

#### how to implement OAUTH2 with spring security, give me detail step by step  

<thinking-content-7bbeb8e1441453ad999a0bbba8a46d4b>

We are going to implement OAuth2 with Spring Security. Since the user has provided a set of files that are not to be edited and only for reference, and we have not been asked to modify any specific file, I will provide a general step-by-step guide. However, note that the user's context includes a lot of JavaScript files (Node.js based) that are about OAuth2 as well, but the question is about Java and Spring Security.

 Steps to implement OAuth2 with Spring Security:

 1. **Add Dependencies**: Include Spring Security OAuth2 dependencies in your `pom.xml` (for Maven) or `build.gradle` (for Gradle).

 For Maven:
 ```xml
 <dependency>
     <groupId>org.springframework.boot</groupId>
     <artifactId>spring-boot-starter-oauth2-client</artifactId>
 </dependency>
 <dependency>
     <groupId>org.springframework.boot</groupId>
     <artifactId>spring-boot-starter-security</artifactId>
 </dependency>
 ```

 2. **Configure OAuth2 Client Properties**:
    In your `application.yml` or `application.properties`, configure the OAuth2 client. Here is an example for Google:

    ```yaml
    spring:
      security:
        oauth2:
          client:
            registration:
              google:
                client-id: <your-client-id>
                client-secret: <your-client-secret>
                scope: 
                  - email
                  - profile
            provider:
              google:
                authorization-uri: https://accounts.google.com/o/oauth2/v2/auth
                token-uri: https://oauth2.googleapis.com/token
                user-info-uri: https://openidconnect.googleapis.com/v1/userinfo
    ```

 3. **Create a Security Configuration Class**:
    Create a class that extends `WebSecurityConfigurerAdapter` (for Spring Boot 2.x) or use a `SecurityFilterChain` bean (for Spring Boot 2.7+ or 3.x). 

    Example for Spring Boot 2.7+ (with Jakarta EE 9):

    ```java
    import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
    import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
    import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.builders.HttpSecurity;
    import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.configuration.EnableWebSecurity;
    import org.springframework.security.web.SecurityFilterChain;

    @Configuration
    @EnableWebSecurity
    public class SecurityConfig {

        @Bean
        public SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
            http
                .authorizeRequests(authorize -> authorize
                    .antMatchers("/", "/login**", "/error**").permitAll()
                    .anyRequest().authenticated()
                )
                .oauth2Login(oauth2 -> oauth2
                    .loginPage("/login")
                    .defaultSuccessUrl("/home", true)
                );
            return http.build();
        }
    }
    ```

 4. **Create a Controller**:
    Create a controller to handle the login and home pages.

    ```java
    import org.springframework.stereotype.Controller;
    import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.GetMapping;

    @Controller
    public class LoginController {

        @GetMapping("/login")
        public String login() {
            return "login";
        }

        @GetMapping("/home")
        public String home() {
            return "home";
        }
    }
    ```

 5. **Create Login Page (if needed)**:
    You can create a custom login page in `src/main/resources/templates/login.html` (if using Thymeleaf) or in the static resources.

 6. **Understand the Flow**:
    - The user accesses a secured resource and is redirected to `/login`.
    - The user clicks on an OAuth2 provider (e.g., Google) and is redirected to the provider's authorization page.
    - After authentication, the provider redirects back to the application with an authorization code.
    - The application exchanges the code for an access token and then uses it to get the user's details.

 7. **Customizing User Info**:
    You can create a custom `OAuth2UserService` to process the user info and map it to your application's user model.

    ```java
    @Service
    public class CustomOAuth2UserService extends DefaultOAuth2UserService {

        @Override
        public OAuth2User loadUser(OAuth2UserRequest userRequest) throws OAuth2AuthenticationException {
            OAuth2User user = super.loadUser(userRequest);
            // Process the user object and return a custom user
            return new CustomOAuth2User(user);
        }
    }
    ```

    Then register this service in your security configuration:

    ```java
    @Autowired
    private CustomOAuth2UserService customOAuth2UserService;

    @Bean
    public SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        http
            .authorizeRequests(authorize -> authorize
                .anyRequest().authenticated()
            )
            .oauth2Login(oauth2 -> oauth2
                .userInfoEndpoint().userService(customOAuth2UserService)
            );
        return http.build();
    }
    ```

 8. **Handling Logout**:
    For OAuth2 logout, you might want to revoke the token and end the session. You can configure logout in the `SecurityFilterChain`:

    ```java
    http.logout(logout -> logout
        .logoutSuccessUrl("/")
        .invalidateHttpSession(true)
        .clearAuthentication(true)
        .deleteCookies("JSESSIONID")
    );
    ```

 9. **Running the Application**:
    Run the Spring Boot application and test by accessing a secured endpoint.

 Note: This is a high-level guide. The exact steps might vary based on the Spring Boot version and the OAuth2 provider you are using.

 Since the user has not specified a particular file to modify, and we are only providing a guide, we are not writing any code to files at this point.

 If the user requires modifications to specific files, they should add those files to the chat and specify the changes.

 Let me know if you need further assistance.


</think>

To implement OAuth2 with Spring Security, follow these structured steps:

### 1. **Dependencies**
Add to `pom.xml`:
```xml
<dependencies>
    <!-- Spring Security OAuth2 -->
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.springframework.boot</groupId>
        <artifactId>spring-boot-starter-oauth2-client</artifactId>
    </dependency>
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.springframework.boot</groupId>
        <artifactId>spring-boot-starter-security</artifactId>
    </dependency>
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.springframework.boot</groupId>
        <artifactId>spring-boot-starter-web</artifactId>
    </dependency>
</dependencies>
```

### 2. **Configuration**
Create `SecurityConfig.java`:
```java
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.builders.HttpSecurity;
import org.springframework.security.web.SecurityFilterChain;

@EnableWebSecurity
public class SecurityConfig {

    @Bean
    public SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        http
            .authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
                .anyRequest().authenticated()
            )
            .oauth2Login(oauth2 -> oauth2
                .defaultSuccessUrl("/", true)
            );
        return http.build();
    }
}
```

### 3. **OAuth2 Provider Setup**
In `application.properties`:
```properties
# Google example
spring.security.oauth2.client.registration.google.client-id=YOUR_CLIENT_ID
spring.security.oauth2.client.registration.google.client-secret=YOUR_CLIENT_SECRET
spring.security.oauth2.client.registration.google.scope=email,profile

# Other providers (GitHub, Facebook etc.) follow same pattern
```

### 4. **User Information Handling**
Create a `CustomOAuth2UserService` to process user data:
```java
@Service
public class CustomOAuth2UserService extends DefaultOAuth2UserService {

    @Override
    public OAuth2User loadUser(OAuth2UserRequest userRequest) {
        OAuth2User user = super.loadUser(userRequest);
        // Extract attributes like email, name from 'user.getAttributes()'
        return new DefaultOAuth2User(
            user.getAuthorities(),
            user.getAttributes(),
            "name" // Key for username attribute (e.g., "sub" or "name")
        );
    }
}
```

### 5. **Controller**
Create endpoints to handle authenticated requests:
```java
@RestController
public class MainController {

    @GetMapping("/")
    public String home(Principal principal) {
        return "Welcome, " + principal.getName();
    }
}
```

### 6. **Key Configurations**
- **Redirect URI**: Register `http://localhost:8080/login/oauth2/code/google` in your OAuth provider dashboard.
- **Session Management**: Spring Security handles sessions/cookies automatically.

### 7. **Run and Test**
Start the application:
1. Access any URL (e.g., `http://localhost:8080`)
2. Auto-redirected to provider's login
3. Redirect back to `/` after auth

### For Authorization Server/Bare Metal Solutions:
If you need a full **OAuth2 Authorization Server**, use Spring Authorization Server project:
```xml
<dependency>
    <groupId>org.springframework.security</groupId>
    <artifactId>spring-security-oauth2-authorization-server</artifactId>
    <version>1.0.2</version>
</dependency>
```

### Notes:
- Test with providers like Google/GitHub first
- Use HTTPS in production
- Map user attributes appropriately for your app domain
- For PKCE, configure clients with:
  ```properties
  spring.security.oauth2.client.registration.google.client-authentication-method=POST
  ```

Add me the relevant files (Java config files) for detailed implementation tweaks!

#### dont roll your auth, what's your opinion about that ?  

<thinking-content-7bbeb8e1441453ad999a0bbba8a46d4b>

We are discussing the principle "Don't roll your own auth" in the context of authentication and authorization systems.

 Key points to cover:
 1. Security Risks: Custom auth systems are prone to critical vulnerabilities (e.g., insecure password storage, session fixation, insufficient rate limiting).
 2. Complexity: Implementing a secure auth system requires deep expertise in cryptography, standards, and attack mitigation.
 3. Maintenance: Ongoing security updates are needed to address new threats and vulnerabilities.
 4. Alternatives: Use well-established, audited libraries/frameworks (like Spring Security, Passport.js, Auth0, Ory, Keycloak) that comply with standards (OAuth 2.0, OpenID Connect, SAML).

 However, there are contexts where custom auth might be necessary (very specific requirements), but it should be done with extreme caution and peer review.

 Since we are in a Spring context, I will emphasize using Spring Security for Java applications.

 Let me structure the response step-by-step using the sequentialthinking MCP tool.

 We are only generating thoughts, not executing an action.


</think>

My expert opinion on "don't roll your own auth" follows this structured reasoning:

### Step-by-Step Analysis
1. **Security Risk Assessment**  
   Authentication/authorization is a critical attack surface. Common vulnerabilities in custom implementations include:  
   - Insecure password storage (plaintext/weak hashing)
   - Broken session management
   - Inadequate brute-force protection
   - Token leakage vulnerabilities

2. **Complexity Justification**  
   Modern auth requires:  
   - OAuth2/OpenID Connect flows
   - Cryptographic signature verification
   - Token revocation mechanisms
   - Compliance with standards (RFC 6749, RFC 7519)  
   *Implementing these correctly is non-trivial.*

3. **Maintenance Burden**  
   Security is moving target:  
   - New vulnerabilities discovered monthly (JWT flaws, protocol bypasses)
   - Rotating cryptographic standards
   - Regulatory requirements (GDPR, PCI-DSS)

4. **Battle-Tested Alternatives**  
   Preferred solutions:  
   ```mermaid
   graph LR
   A[Use Cases] --> B[Spring Security]
   A --> C[Auth0/Authing]
   A --> D[Keycloak]
   A --> E[Amazon Cognito]
   ```

### Final Recommendation
✅ **Don't roll your own auth** unless:  
- You're building an auth product
- Have dedicated security team
- Can afford third-party audits

For Spring applications:  
```java
// Instead of custom auth:
@EnableWebSecurity
public class SecurityConfig {
    // Use framework-provided OIDC/OAuth2
}

// Instead of:
// CustomFilter ← Danger Zone!
```

Exceptions where limited customization is acceptable:  
- Adding domain-specific claims to JWTs  
- Custom authorization rules *on top of* Spring Security's ACL system

What's your specific use case? I can recommend appropriate auth strategies.

Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize finance by solving complex problems that are currently intractable for classical computers. Some key areas where quantum computing is applied in finance include:

- **Portfolio Optimization**: Quantum algorithms can efficiently find the optimal portfolio allocation by evaluating a vast number of combinations of assets.
- **Risk Analysis**: Quantum computers can simulate complex financial systems and compute risk metrics faster.
- **Option Pricing**: Quantum algorithms can compute the price of derivatives more accurately by using quantum versions of Monte Carlo simulations.
- **Fraud Detection**: Quantum machine learning can process large datasets to detect patterns indicative of fraud.

However, quantum computing is still in its early stages. Current quantum computers have limited qubits and are prone to errors, so practical applications are under development.

Quantum computing has several promising applications in finance, leveraging its ability to solve complex problems exponentially faster than classical computers in certain domains:

1. **Portfolio Optimization**: 
   - Quantum computing can efficiently analyze vast combinations of assets to find optimal portfolios that maximize returns while minimizing risk. Classical computers struggle with these NP-hard problems as complexity grows.

2. **Risk Analysis**:
   - Enables rapid Monte Carlo simulations for market risk assessment, value-at-risk (VaR) calculations, and counterparty risk modeling, reducing computation times from hours to seconds.

3. **Option Pricing & Derivatives**:
   - Quantum algorithms like Amplitude Estimation accelerate options pricing models (e.g. Black-Scholes, Monte Carlo methods) with quadratic speedups.

4. **Arbitrage Opportunities**:
   - Quickly identifies pricing inconsistencies across markets/asset classes by solving combinatorial optimization problems in real-time.

5. **Fraud Detection**:
   - Quantum machine learning models can detect complex financial fraud patterns in high-dimensional datasets more effectively.

6. **Credit Scoring**:
   - Processes non-traditional variables (e.g., transaction networks) for more nuanced risk assessments.

7. **Cryptography**:
   - Quantum-resistant cryptography is being developed to secure financial systems against future quantum attacks on current encryption standards.

**Current State**:
- Experimental stage - IBM, Goldman Sachs, JP Morgan, and fintech startups are running early pilots
- Hybrid quantum-classical approaches currently predominate
- Challenged by qubit stability (decoherence) and error rates
- Financial quantum advantage expected in specialized applications within 5-10 years

3. Explain specific algorithms like QAOA or quantum annealing?
Let me know your focus.

#### is there any skill sets do I need to have to use quantum, to help me analyze stocks and create better portofolio  

<thinking-content-7bbeb8e1441453ad999a0bbba8a46d4b>

 The user asks: "is there any skill sets do I need to have to use quantum, to help me analyze stocks and create better portfolio"

 Answer:

 To effectively use quantum computing for stock analysis and portfolio optimization, you'll need a combination of skills:

 1. **Quantum Computing Fundamentals**:
    - Understanding of qubits, superposition, entanglement, and quantum gates
    - Knowledge of quantum algorithms (e.g., Shor, Grover, QAOA, VQE)

 2. **Quantum Programming**:
    - Proficiency with quantum development kits (Qiskit, Cirq, Pennylane)
    - Experience running algorithms on simulators and real quantum hardware

 3. **Classical Finance Knowledge**:
    - Portfolio theory (Modern Portfolio Theory - MPT)
    - Risk management (Value at Risk - VaR)
    - Options pricing models (Black-Scholes)
    - Asset allocation strategies

 4. **Mathematical Foundations**:
    - Linear algebra
    - Probability and statistics
    - Optimization techniques (especially combinatorial optimization)

 5. **Hybrid Quantum-Classical Algorithm Design**:
    - Ability to map financial problems to quantum circuits
    - Experience with variational algorithms (like QAOA for portfolio optimization)

 6. **Data Science and Machine Learning**:
    - Time series analysis for stock data
    - Feature engineering and dimensionality reduction
    - Classical ML techniques that can integrate quantum layers (e.g., quantum neural networks)

 7. **Software Engineering**:
    - Integrating quantum code with existing financial data pipelines
    - Cloud platforms (IBM Quantum Experience, AWS Braket, Azure Quantum)

 8. **Quantum Error Mitigation**:
    - Techniques to handle noise in current NISQ devices

 Since direct quantum advantage is still emerging in finance, you should also:

 9. **Stay Current with Research**:
    - Follow quantum finance papers and industry case studies (like the ones from JPMorgan, Goldman Sachs)

 10. **Networking**:
    - Engage with quantum computing communities (like Qiskit Slack) and finance technology groups

 Given your background, I recommend starting with:

 - Online courses: IBM's Qiskit tutorials, edX quantum computing programs
 - Study quantum algorithms for finance: Portfolio Optimization using QAOA, Quantum Monte Carlo for pricing
 - Learn hybrid frameworks: Qiskit Finance, PennyLane's portfolio optimization example

 Let me know if you'd like to see an example QAOA portfolio optimization circuit! Or I can look for learning resources with `brave_search`.


</think>

To effectively use quantum computing for stock analysis and portfolio optimization, you'll need a diverse skill set spanning several domains:

1. **Quantum Computing Fundamentals**:
- Qubits, superposition, and entanglement
- Quantum gates and circuits
- Algorithm design (Grover, Shor, QAOA, VQE)

2. **Programming and Tools**:
- Python with quantum libraries (Qiskit, Cirq, Pennylane)
- Jupyter notebooks for experimentation
- Hybrid classical-quantum programming

3. **Financial Domain Knowledge**:
- Modern Portfolio Theory (MPT)
- Risk-return frameworks (VaR, CVaR)
- Asset pricing models
- Time series analysis

4. **Mathematics**:
- Linear algebra (matrix operations)
- Probability and statistics
- Optimization (linear/quadratic programming)
- Stochastic calculus

5. **Quantum Finance Specializations**:
- Mapping financial problems to quantum Hamiltonians
- Quantum sampling techniques
- Quantum machine learning (QML)
- Error mitigation strategies

For practical applications with current near-term quantum devices, focus first on hybrid quantum-classical algorithms like QAOA. Here's a simple pseudocode example for portfolio optimization:

```python
# Quantum Portfolio Optimization with QAOA
def quantum_portfolio_optimization(assets, returns, covariances):
    # Convert to Quadratic Unconstrained Binary Optimization (QUBO)
    qubo = build_qubo(returns, covariances, risk_factor)
    
    # Set up QAOA circuit
    num_qubits = len(assets)
    mixer_hamiltonian = compute_mixer(qubo)
    cost_hamiltonian = compute_cost(qubo)
    
    # Initialize parameters
    params = initial_params()
    
    # Classical optimization loop
    def objective(params):
        quantum_circuit = qaoa_circuit(cost_hamiltonian, mixer_hamiltonian, params)
        bitstrings = execute(circuit, shots=1000).result()
        mean, std = evaluate_performance(bitstrings, returns, covariances)
        return -mean  # Maximize returns
    
    # Find optimal parameters
    opt_params = classical_optimizer(objective, params)
    
    # Get final portfolio
    solution = execute(optimal_circuit).top_solution()
    return select_assets(solution)
```

 ```
   var query = requests[reqid];

     // there was no matching saved request, this is an error

       codes[code] = { request: query };
 ```
